<div id="review_1444270890" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1444270890" />
    <a title="Davyne DeSye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49054501-davyne-desye"><img alt="Davyne DeSye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538613956p2/49054501.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1444270890?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Davyne DeSye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Davyne DeSye" href="/user/show/49054501-davyne-desye">Davyne DeSye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1444270890" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7853564523749266170">I just love this story – it is my all-time favorite of Poe’s works. This probably has something to do with the tender age at which I first read it (and the creep factor it engendered in me), but I still consider it my favorite and extremely creepy!<br/><br/>Poe, like Ray Bradbury (another favorite of mine), lets the reader’s imagination fill in the blanks. Yet, even while allowing the reader’s creative vision to complete the picture, I love the details of the setting that Poe gives in this story. I can pi</span>
  <span id="freeText7853564523749266170" style="display:none">I just love this story – it is my all-time favorite of Poe’s works. This probably has something to do with the tender age at which I first read it (and the creep factor it engendered in me), but I still consider it my favorite and extremely creepy!<br /><br />Poe, like Ray Bradbury (another favorite of mine), lets the reader’s imagination fill in the blanks. Yet, even while allowing the reader’s creative vision to complete the picture, I love the details of the setting that Poe gives in this story. I can picture the costumes, the party, the character of the unfortunate Fortunado, and the catacombs that house the wine cellar. As Montresor leads Fortunado deeper and deeper into the cellar, simply through the use of language, the chill beneath the skin on my arms begins to build.<br /><br />And the ending, whoa. That it didn’t give me nightmares when I read it as a child is amazing (although there is absolutely nothing graphic included – which I suppose answers that question), as the visions/sounds/surroundings brought to mind by the ending are still chilling to me now. Absolutely wonderful! Especially to a person who doesn’t care for gory or explicit horror. Instead, this story elicits dread which, to my mind, is as close as I want to get to true horror.<br /><br />Highly recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7853564523749266170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3073303800" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3073303800" />
    <a title="Connie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g"><img alt="Connie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3073303800?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie G" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g">Connie G</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer11265073323542512293">The opening sentence grabs the reader: "The thousand injuries of Fortunato I had borne as I best could; but when he ventured upon insult, I vowed revenge." It is carnival season and Montressor, the narrator, has purchased a cask of Amontillado that he wants Fortunato to evaluate. He is able to manipulate Fortunato because he is very proud of being a wine connoisseur. Montressor leads him to the place where he will enact his revenge. The atmosphere is cold, damp, dark, and macabre. As they walk t</span>
  <span id="freeText11265073323542512293" style="display:none">The opening sentence grabs the reader: "The thousand injuries of Fortunato I had borne as I best could; but when he ventured upon insult, I vowed revenge." It is carnival season and Montressor, the narrator, has purchased a cask of Amontillado that he wants Fortunato to evaluate. He is able to manipulate Fortunato because he is very proud of being a wine connoisseur. Montressor leads him to the place where he will enact his revenge. The atmosphere is cold, damp, dark, and macabre. As they walk the sound of Fortunato's bells from his jester's costume contrast with the evil in Montressor's heart. This is a tightly written tale of terror that sent chills through me.</span>

<div id="review_1614999368" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1614999368" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1614999368?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781583415801" href="/book/show/3016963-the-cask-of-amontillado">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=darkness-horror-gothic">darkness-horror-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=mystery-crime-and-thrillers">mystery-crime-and-thrillers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1614999368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4475807953509897370">Poe writes like a man obsessed. His stories linger on recurring fears, and the subsequent deaths caused by paranoia. This one felt claustrophobic, like it was enclosed and encased in a veil of darkness and petty revenge. As soon as the characters entered the eerie catacombs it became apparent where this tale was going; it was like a big spoiler: it became obvious that only one would leave alive because that’s Poe for you. <br/><br/>Try to resist his words:<br/><br/><i> “A succession of loud and shrill screams, burstin</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4475807953509897370" style="display:none">Poe writes like a man obsessed. His stories linger on recurring fears, and the subsequent deaths caused by paranoia. This one felt claustrophobic, like it was enclosed and encased in a veil of darkness and petty revenge. As soon as the characters entered the eerie catacombs it became apparent where this tale was going; it was like a big spoiler: it became obvious that only one would leave alive because that’s Poe for you. <br><br>Try to resist his words:<br><br><i> “A succession of loud and shrill screams, bursting suddenly from the throat of the chained form, seemed to thrust me violently back. For a brief moment I hesitated, I trembled. Unsheathing my rapier, I began to grope with it about the recess; but the thought of an instant reassured me. I placed my hand upon the solid fabric of the catacombs, and felt satisfied. I reapproached the wall; I replied to the yells of him who clamoured. I re-echoed, I aided, I surpassed them in volume and in strength. I did this, and the clamourer grew still.”</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1461087136i/18822693._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Spoilers coming. <br><br>He has a wonderful ways of putting words together, a way that is completely enthralling. I love the way that he writes. His characters are usually really complex too. Poe uses many different reasons to justify murder. Well, at least to the perpetrator. He explains their complex psychology, and the madness that resulted in such a deed. This one, however, appeared rather normal. The narrator is pissed off with Fortuna. He tries to sell Fortuna some cheap knock off wine; it isn’t a cask of Amontillado. He feels like a fool and wants some revenge for some vague reason. By Poe’s standard this is all very mundane. This is a guy who has just ripping of another guy. <br><br>As the story progresses it becomes much darker. Montresor is the real case study. He claims that Fortuna has insulted him beyond reason; he has given him a thousand injuries. But there is no such proof to the claim. All we see is one man systematically plan the murder of another. There is no reliability in the claim or the story itself. It’s a killing that is covered up by a vague and weak excuse. Perhaps Montresor invented the insult to give him an excuse to indulge in his dark passion. Perhaps he has gone mad, and has perceived insults that were not there. Either way it ends with one man buried behind a wall, and another perpetual mystery raised by the enigmatic Poe. <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="4475807953509897370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13617055038196191355">I have read this story probably a dozen times and today I realized something I had missed in all those readings--they were young. Montresor and Fortunato were young men when this happened. And that has changed my entire perception of this story, making it even more horrible than it has ever been to me.<br/><br/>One of my favorite Poe stories, I find it so revealing of both men's characters. False pride and hubris drive them both. I marvel at how Poe can not only sketch a story, but also a character, in so</span>
  <span id="freeText13617055038196191355" style="display:none">I have read this story probably a dozen times and today I realized something I had missed in all those readings--they were young. Montresor and Fortunato were young men when this happened. And that has changed my entire perception of this story, making it even more horrible than it has ever been to me.<br /><br />One of my favorite Poe stories, I find it so revealing of both men's characters. False pride and hubris drive them both. I marvel at how Poe can not only sketch a story, but also a character, in so few pages.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18202460721865420901">I didn't seem to learn my lesson. Do not listen to Edgar Allan Poe audiobook when you think you are alone! It will give you arrhythmia. For sure.<br/><br/>Yeah, I did that (again) during lunch break, it was quite, so I took my phone, put my earphones and listen to this, The Cask of Amontadillo (I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7810380.The_Sherlockian' title='The Sherlockian by Graham Moore' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sherlockian</a> a while ago and it was mention by ACD character, hence 'curious').   <span id="freeText18202460721865420901" style="display:none">I didn't seem to learn my lesson. Do not listen to Edgar Allan Poe audiobook when you think you are alone! It will give you arrhythmia. For sure.<br /><br />Yeah, I did that (again) during lunch break, it was quite, so I took my phone, put my earphones and listen to this, The Cask of Amontadillo (I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7810380.The_Sherlockian" title="The Sherlockian by Graham Moore" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sherlockian</a> a while ago and it was mention by ACD character, hence 'curious'). Closed my eyes... and listened.<br /><br />Arrhythmia!!<br /><br />The story itself was rather straight point. Fortnato did something that insult Montressor. Montressor vow for revenge. But Poe wrote it so meticulously and so tautly, so the tense was built up little by little, get higher everytime they descend the wine vault... until....<br /><br /><i> "For the love of God, Montresor!"</i><br /><br />For the love of God, indeed.<br /><br />Nemo me impune lacessit ~ <i>No one attacks me with impunity</i><br /><br /><br /><br />NB. I got the audiobook from Librivox, and it was outstanding, with sound fx and all. Love it.<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.archive.org/download/stories_002_librivox/cask_of_amontillado_poe_zw-rh.mp3">http://www.archive.org/download/stori...</a></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3113167241" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3113167241" />
    <a title="Jeanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7743315-jeanne"><img alt="Jeanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488125474p2/7743315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3113167241?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeanne" href="/user/show/7743315-jeanne">Jeanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7743315-jeanne?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7743315-jeanne?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7743315-jeanne?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7743315-jeanne?shelf=gothic-horror">gothic-horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3113167241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14594060353707131735">Montressor was a patient man – of sorts – and waited to exact his punishment after 1000 injuries and one insult. He wanted revenge with impunity. What did Fortunato do? We don't know but, like many of us, Montressor felt that his punishment was justified and appropriate to the crime. Montressor observed, <em>"A wrong is unredressed when retribution overtakes its redresser. It is equally unredressed when the avenger fails to make himself felt as such to him who has done the wrong"</em> (Kindle 11975-11976</span>
  <span id="freeText14594060353707131735" style="display:none">Montressor was a patient man – of sorts – and waited to exact his punishment after 1000 injuries and one insult. He wanted revenge with impunity. What did Fortunato do? We don't know but, like many of us, Montressor felt that his punishment was justified and appropriate to the crime. Montressor observed, <em>"A wrong is unredressed when retribution overtakes its redresser. It is equally unredressed when the avenger fails to make himself felt as such to him who has done the wrong"</em> (Kindle 11975-11976). <br><br>In his thoughtful review, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/830459285?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Glenn Russell</a> used Schopenhauer's views of revenge to consider <em>The Cask of Amontillado</em>. In <em>The Horrors and Absurdities of Religion</em> (1851), Schopenhauer posited that suffering is a constant in life, but suffering at the hand of someone else is peculiarly injurious, as it points to the injurer's apparent superiority and one's own impotence. Mere recompense does not even the balance:<br><br>"if recompense is possible, [it] can cure the injury done: but that bitter addition, the feeling 'and that is what I have to put up with from you' which often hurts more than the injury itself, can be neutralized only by revenge." (Schopenhauer, 1851/2009) <br><br>We want the balance evened. This cannot be just an eye for an eye, but must include compensation for our pain and suffering. <br><br>In <em>The Cask of Amontillado</em>, we never learn what the thousand injuries are, nor the single insult. Instead, Montressor focused on Fortunato's comeuppance. Throughout, Montressor is like the angler fish, enticing his unsuspecting victim to his death, always appearing concerned about poor Fortunato's health, while enticing him further into the catacombs' webs. Tell us not to do something and our barriers drop, and we want to do it.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1577903904i/28698410._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <a href="https://pixels.com/featured/angler-fish-aaron-spong.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Source</a>: Aaron Spong<br><br>Alfred Hitchcock – or perhaps Edward Bergen – said, “Revenge is sweet and not fattening.” Perhaps this reflects my own morality, but I don't believe that revenge is a zero sum game. We believe revenge is sweet, but Eadah, Peak, and Lambert (<a href="https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0022103116302554" rel="nofollow noopener">2017</a>) demonstrated that revenge is both sweet <em>and</em> bitter. Although insults require additional payment to feel "even"; revenge has a cost to the revenger. Montressor has given up his soul to kill Fortunato in such a heartless and dispassionate way.<br><br>Montressor perceived no cost and was almost gleeful throughout. His gleefulness and apparent lack of remorse or punishment afterward make <em>The Cask of Amontillado</em> a morally-ambiguous tale. As a result, I find Poe's <em>The Tell-tale Heart</em> an easier, less discomfiting read.<br><br>Montressor, like the narrator in <em>The Tell-tale Heart</em>, has little insight, at least relative to his crime. If there were no cost to his revenge, would Montressor's heart grow sick (albeit, on account of the dampness of the catacombs)? Would he still look back and think about his murder from a half century later?<br><br>I read this as part of the Catching up on Classics (and lots more!) group.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14594060353707131735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('irAWCsd1KdnwFYsEYVozEgDMnhRFzR1mXSs9oQgyMG6oG+T1thTQYED1SICUg6zNJI/nIZf2uUj5fmuk4f0+uw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('l9S2TnJfXorcCXCNQ3HWbn/YpRRoP5A1MhOVIBFaGoa1f0SxAz6nM2zpswm2qEmxW5vcIboENBuWRsMl+JUUUw==')})
  }

More than anything else O\'Connor ever wrote, this story mixes the comedy, violence, and religious concerns that characterize her fiction.\n\nThis casebook for the story includes an introduction by the editor, a chronology of the author\'s life, the authoritative text of the story itself, comments and letters by O\'Connor about the story, critical essays, and a bibliography.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">About Edgar Allan Poe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1454522972p3/4624490.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">Edgar Allan Poe
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
20,394
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10288578806274998133">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of</span>
  <span id="freeText10288578806274998133" style="display:none">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of essays and book reviews. He is widely acknowledged as the inventor of the modern detective story and an innovator in the science fiction genre, but he made his living as America’s first great literary critic and theoretician. Poe’s reputation today rests primarily on his tales of terror as well as on his haunting lyric poetry.<br /><br />Just as the bizarre characters in Poe’s stories have captured the public imagination so too has Poe himself. He is seen as a morbid, mysterious figure lurking in the shadows of moonlit cemeteries or crumbling castles. This is the Poe of legend. But much of what we know about Poe is wrong, the product of a biography written by one of his enemies in an attempt to defame the author’s name.<br /><br />The real Poe was born to traveling actors in Boston on January 19, 1809. Edgar was the second of three children. His other brother William Henry Leonard Poe would also become a poet before his early death, and Poe’s sister Rosalie Poe would grow up to teach penmanship at a Richmond girls’ school. Within three years of Poe’s birth both of his parents had died, and he was taken in by the wealthy tobacco merchant John Allan and his wife Frances Valentine Allan in Richmond, Virginia while Poe’s siblings went to live with other families. Mr. Allan would rear Poe to be a businessman and a Virginia gentleman, but Poe had dreams of being a writer in emulation of his childhood hero the British poet Lord Byron. Early poetic verses found written in a young Poe’s handwriting on the backs of Allan’s ledger sheets reveal how little interest Poe had in the tobacco business.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_allan_poe">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_al...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10288578806274998133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
